security || This patch is (A) a security patch (SRP). 

The patch addresses a security vulnerability where a malicious user could provoke mkstemp failure and race to create a symlink with the selected temporary file name, thus causing the qemu process (usually root owned) to open through the symlink, overwriting an attacker-chosen file. This vulnerability is identified as CVE-2012-2652, which is a security vulnerability identifier. 

The patch fixes this by changing the get_tmp_filename function to return an int (not void), so that it can inform its two callers of those failures. It also avoids the risk of buffer overrun and does not ignore mkstemp or close failure. 

Therefore, this patch is a security patch as it addresses a specific security vulnerability.

non-security || This patch is a security patch (SRP). The patch is fixing a segmentation fault (seg fault) issue which is a specific kind of error caused by accessing memory that "does not belong to you." It's a mechanism that prevents you from corrupting the memory and introducing hard-to-debug memory bugs. Whenever you get a seg fault, you know you are doing something wrong with memory - such as accessing a variable that has already been freed or writing to a read-only portion of memory. The implications of such bugs can be serious in terms of security depending on the context. Therefore, this patch can be classified as a security patch.

non-security || This patch is a (B) non-security patch (NSP). 

The patch is addressing an issue with memory management related to dirty page migration in the memory cgroup (memcg) subsystem of the Linux kernel. It is not directly related to a security vulnerability, memory leak, or null pointer problem. Instead, it is fixing a performance issue where the dirty page count in a memcg can underflow, leading to aggressive throttling of buffered writes by processes in non-root memcgs. This can harm the performance of non-root memcg buffered writes and report too small (even negative) values in memory.stat[(total_)dirty] counters of all memcgs, including the root. 

The patch introduces two helper functions, page_memcg() and set_page_memcg(), to avoid polluting migrate.c with #ifdef CONFIG_MEMCG checks. It also modifies the move_to_new_page() function in migrate.c to use these new helper functions to correctly manage the memcg dirty page accounting during page migration. 

The patch does not mention or imply any security vulnerability, memory leak, or null pointer problem. Therefore, it is a non-security patch.

However, there is no direct mention or implication of a security vulnerability, memory leak, or null pointer problem being addressed. Therefore, it is not a security patch.

However, the patch does not directly mention or address "memory leak" or "null pointer" problems. Therefore, based on the information provided, this patch would be classified as a (B) non-security patch (NSP).
